==PAGE:3==
Summary
The evolution of embedded systems and particularly of avionics, calls for new
challenges that concern the security of those systems. One of those challenges
is, ensuring the integrity of the communicated data, by enforcing content de-
pendent information ow policies.
In this thesis we have developed a decidable mechanism that enforces content
dependent information ow policies for integrity, using the art of Abstract In-
terpretation. The decidability of our mechanism relies on the use of atomistic
Galois insertions and the choice of nite atomistic complete lattices to express
our security policies.
We start by considering a sequential language, while later on, we extend its syn-
tax with pointers and synchronous communication between processes. Next, we
proof the correctness of our mechanism with respect to a correct static analysis
specication. We nish our work, with a concrete implementation of a tool that
represents our enforcement mechanism. Finally, we test our implementation in
a semi-realistic scenario provided by the aerospace industry and we show our
results.

semantics of programming languages, Abstract Interpretation, and information
ow control.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Embedded systems are the driving force for technology development in many
industries such as the aerospace, the automotive, the healthcare, and the trans-
portation.
Both security and safety analysis of those systems is essential to
ensure their safe operation. Until now a primary focus has been given to the
safety part and less to the security. One crucial security issue of embedded sys-
tems is the protection of the data manipulated by its dierent components. The
security of the communicated data is highly dependent on integrity and con-
dentiality security policies. Several methods that enforce such security policies
in order to limit the information disclosure exist today. One of them is Access
Control [SdV01], which grants or denies access to the data based on the iden-
tity of the user. However, although Access Control does impose limits on the
information that is released by a system, it provides no guarantees about infor-
mation propagation. Information Flow Control [DD77] extends Access Control
by enforcing the programs to adhere to the security policies throughout.
A
classical methodology to impose information ow control is that of the De-
centralized Label Model(DLM) [ML97] [ML00].
However, inside the area of
embedded communications systems, the DLM approach is not sucient. Infor-
mation ow should also be content dependent [HRN15] [HRN16], which means
that the content of the messages which are communicated between the dierent
partitions of the system should also be examined. To illustrate the need for the
content dependent information ow control, we will examine an example of the
aerospace industry, where the avionics software is used to ensure the safety and
==PAGE:14==
2
Introduction
the security of an aircraft's execution during a ight.
1.1
Integrated Modular Avionic
In the past, the avionic system of an aircraft was designed based on the "feder-
ated architecture" principal [HBSA14] [Tri12]. This principle is also known as
the " one device, one function" principle; meaning that every electronic device
of the system was responsible for a specic task, while its interactions with the
other devices of the system were limited, keeping in that way the complexity
of the system to low levels. Therefore the analysis and the verication of the
system's safety was quite simple.
However, modern aircraft require avionics
with more and smarter functionality, such as navigation systems that oer high
precision and automation and systems that provide on ight services to the pas-
sengers such as wi-. The "federated architecture" of the old avionics fails to
meet that requirement since the large set of those devices would consume a vast
amount of power, while the cost of adding and maintaining a new device for all
those functionalities of the system is considered as signicantly high.
The Integrated Modular Avionic (IMA) [HBSA14] [Tri12] was then introduced to
solve this problem. IMA is a distributive real-time computer network consisting
of processing modules, while every processing module hosts dierent software
functions that share computing resources. Hence in order to still allow the anal-
ysis of each function as an individual subsystem, every processing module is
partitioned in terms of resources (e.g I/O, memory resources). However, until
now avionics were used to ensure the safe execution of the dierent applica-
tions of a plane, the new IMA concept of shared resources and communication
between the dierent applications of the aircraft brings a new set of concerns
regarding the security of the functions, used by those applications. That issue is
referred as "security for safety" [Tri12]. For instance consider a scenario, where
an application AE which is responsible for the passenger's entertainment was
possessed by an intruder, who could then inuence the data received by the
aircraft control system CS, through the communication network that AE and
CS are connected to. This scenario could have catastrophic consequences.
1.2
Security policies
Security of systems though has various interpretations. For instance, the se-
curity of an IMA system could be described as an information ow problem
==PAGE:15==
1.3 Multiple Independent Levels of Security
3
and hence a security policy has to be imposed on a system, describing how the
information should ow from one component of the system to the other.
A
common approach, that imposes condentiality security policies to systems is
the BellLaPadula [DBF73]. BellLaPadula assigns a security level (e.g Low,
High) to each software component of the system. A hierarchy then can be im-
posed on the dierent components of the systems (e.g Low is below High), and
then the security policy states that a component C1 with security level lower
than a component C2 (e.g C1:Low, C2:High) should not read information from
C2; while C2 should not write information to C1. Similarly to BellLaPadula,
the Biba [Bib77] approach enforces integrity security policies to a system by
assigning security levels to the dierent software components of the system (e.g
Untrusted, Trusted). Then for a component C1 with security level lower than a
component C2 (e.g C1:Untrusted, C2 :Trusted), Biba states that C1 should not
write information to C2 and C2 should not read information from C1.
Unfortunately, those approaches were too rigid to be used in practice.
The
decentralized label model (DLM) [ML97] [ML00] approach was then introduced
by Andrew C. Myers, making the control of information ow more exible. In
DLM we assume that there is a nite set of security principals; for instance in
an IMA that could be the set of the dierent functions used by the software
components of the system. Then for some data x and two set of security prin-
cipals O, S an integrity policy is described as a label tO Ð Su x, stating that
the principals in O are the owners of x, allow the principals in S to inuence x.
While a condentiality policy tO Ñ Su x ,states that the principals in O, who
own x, allow the principals in S to read x. The novelty of the DLM approach
was the introduction of the two operations declassication and endorsement.
The declassication operation can be used on a condentiality policy, resulting
in a weaker policy by removing an owner o or adding a reader s. Similarly,
the endorsement operation is used for the integrity policies and it can "relax"
a policy by removing an owner or an inuencer of the policy. However, the use
of those two operations is not encouraged since they bypass the formal security
policies of a system.
1.3
Multiple Independent Levels of Security
As it has been already mentioned, ensuring the security of an IMA is not an easy
task. In the ARCINC-811 report [oOF05], the security issues that arise in the
avionic systems are addressed, leading to the need for separating the components
of an avionic system into dierent security domains. Multiple independent levels
of security (MILS) was rst introduced by John Rushby in 1981 [Rus08] [Rus81].
The design of a system that meets the MILS specication is described in two
==PAGE:16==
4
Introduction
steps. The rst step is that a distributive system has to be split into a set of
sub-components in a way that every one of those components is as simple as
possible, while the second step is that the dierent resources (e.g data, memory,
network) of the system are assigned to those components. In that way is easier to
describe a security policy for each one of those stand-alone, simple components.
A separation kernel then is used to assure that all those security policies are
enforced correctly to the system, whenever a component communicates with
other components of the system, or requests access to a resource of the system.
1.4
An example of a MILS system
An example of an MILS system is the secure getaway given in [HBSA14], where
the separation kernel PikeOs [Pik] is used to impose information ow control.
However, information ow policies expressed in the traditional DLM fail to
express how the information should ow from one component to the other.
Figure 1.1: A secure getaway
The getaway consists of a six processes.
The producers p0 and p1 produce
some data for the consumers c0, c1 respectively. The processes p0 and p1 send
their data through the channels in0 and in1 respectively to a multiplexer m.
The multiplexer then forwards the data to the demultiplexer through a dyadic
channel ch. The channel ch here carries two kind of information, which are
stored in the channel's ch variables #1, #2 ; the value of #1 corresponds to the
data sent by m, while the value of #2 acts like a mark, identifying the sender (p0
or p1 in our case) of the data stored in #1. The values of #1, #2 are then stored
in the variables z and y respectively and then the demultiplexer distributes z
to c0 or c1, according to the value of y which species the source of the data.
In this scenario we want to specify a policy, which expresses that the data sent
by p0 reaches only c0, while the data sent by p1 reaches only c1. Clearly this
policy is dependent on the value of the variable y. Assume now that we have
the following set of security principals tP0, P1, m, d, C0, C1u. A DLM policy then
==PAGE:17==
1.5 Thesis Overview
5
could be
ttP0, P1, m, d, C0, C1u Ð P0u z
expressing that all of our principals own the variable z and they believe that
P0 may inuence the value of z. Similarly to the previous policy we specify the
policy
ttP0, P1, m, d, C0, C1u Ð P1u z
, talking now about the producer p1.
The system policy then will combine
those two policies, creating the policy ttP0, P1, m, d, C0, C1u Ð P0, P1u z, stat-
ing that the value of z can always be inuenced by P0 and P1 which does not
meet our requirements. Of course, the endorse operation of DLM could be used
here, but then this would require the informal review of the code and the security
of the system would not rely on correctness of the enforcement mechanism.
1.5
Thesis Overview
We specify content dependent information ow policies for integrity using the
art of Abstract Interpretation .Chapter 2 begins with presenting the required
denitions of the Abstract Interpretation in order to specify our policies later.
Next, we describe two dierent ways that someone can represent program states
of a program. We continue by dening standard ways of constructing atomistic
Galois Insertions between a set of program states and an abstract state,while
also between two abstract states. Chapter 2 then ends by giving the formal
denition of a content dependent information ow policy, using an atomistic
Galois insertion, while we also we give a decidable algorithm which compares
two policies.
In Chapter 3 we start by considering a simple sequential language While. We
give its semantics based on program graphs and then we give the denition of
a correct specication of static analysis. Next, we present a mechanism for en-
forcing content dependent information ow policies on a program graph. The
enforcement mechanism uses an atomistic Galois insertion that links abstract
states of an admissible result (with respect to a correct specication of static
analysis) with abstract states that used to specify content dependent information
ow policies. The chapter ends with a theorem that expresses the correctness
of our mechanism.
==PAGE:18==
6
Introduction
In Chapter 4 we extend the While language with commands that allow two
programs to communicate using synchronous communication.
Next, we give
the denition of a distributive system, while the rest of the chapter is organized
as Chapter 3, by starting with giving the semantics of a system and ending with
the correctness of our enforcement mechanism.
In Chapter 5 we extend the language of Chapter 4 with pointers that point
to simple variables. We describe the new information ows that occur due to
the pointers and the rest of the chapter follows the same pattern as Chapter 4.
However our enforcement mechanism here can check only programs that their
admissible result satises a certain condition. This condition concerns the safety
of the system and since we are dealing with the avionics systems, we assume
that our condition holds.
In Chapter 6 we describe a concrete implementation of a tool that enforces
content dependent information ow policies using our enforcement mechanism.
The implementation was made in the functional programming language F#.
In Chapter 7 we give our conclusions. Finally, in Appendix A, B , C, D we
include the proofs of the dierent lemmas, facts, and theorems which were pre-
sented in Chapter 2, 3, 4 and 5 respectively.
1.6
Related Work
The work of this thesis is based on the approaches of [HRN15] and [HRN16].
Both of them enforce content dependent information ow policies on the lan-
guage presented in Chapter 4; while the approach of [HRN15] uses a Hoare logic
and the one of [HRN16] uses Abstract Interpretation techniques . Compared
to [HRN15], we could claim the advantage that our approach uses a decidable
algorithm when it comes to the enforcement mechanism, while the approach
of [HRN15] lacks of it. Regarding the approach of [HRN16] we prove the sound-
ness of our enforcement mechanism, in a dierent way, while also we consider a
richer language that also includes pointers.
The Jif language [o] is an extension of the Java language, which also allows
enforcement of information ow policies. However, avionics use the C language
for their software. An interesting approach is the one of [Mac15], which presents
a tool that enforces content dependent information ow policies on a subset of
==PAGE:19==
1.6 Related Work
7
the C language, using a combination of a Hoare logic and a type system. Still,
there is no proof of the correctness of the type system, while again here we could
claim the advantage of the decidability of our enforcement mechanism. Also, we
believe that our enforcement mechanism could t great in the avionics industry
since they already use the powerful static analyzer Astrée [T], to safely over
approximate behaviors of their programs.
Finally, the approach of [MA] considers a language with pointers and extends
the semantics of the language with a memory that statically assigns security
levels to the locations of a program's variables. Then, a type system enforces
information ow control by checking if there are invalid ows from locations with
low-security level to ones with high. However, their language does not support
concurrency, while also they do not consider content dependent information ow
policies.
==PAGE:20==
8
Introduction
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Content dependent
information ow policies
using Abstract
Interpretation
In this chapter, we present a way of specifying content dependent information
ow policies for integrity using Abstract Interpretation techniques.
We start by giving basic denitions of the Abstract Interpretation which are
essential to understand the rest of the chapter. Next, we present two dierent
methodologies on how to construct an atomistic Galois insertion that relates a
set of a program's states to an abstract program state. In the rst methodology
we consider a state of a program as a mapping from variables to values, while
the second describes a state of a program as a pair of an environment that
maps every variable to a location and a memory which maps every location
to a value or to another location. In addition to those two methodologies we
also present a construction of Galois insertions between two abstract states.
Finally, we present the syntax of a content dependent information ow policy
(for integrity) and we dene a decidable pre-order on the policies, which will be
used later to prove that a program is secure with respect to its security policies.

2.1
Basic Denitions
All of the denitions below have been taken from the book "Principles of Pro-
gram Analysis" [FNH99], however, the proofs of the lemmas and facts of this
chapter have been made by the author of this thesis, in order to acquire the
basic foundations of the Abstract Interpretation.
Partially ordered set. A partial ordering is a binary relation : L  L Ñ
ttrue, falseu that @l1, l2, l3 P L satises :
• l1  l1 (reexivity)
• l1  l2 ^ l2  l1 ñ l1  l2 (antisymmetry)
• l1  l2 ^ l2  l3 ñ l1  l3 (transitivity)
A partially ordered set is pL, q is a set L equipped with a partial ordering .
Upper and Lower bounds. A subset Y of a partially ordered set pL, q has
the element l P L as an upper bound if
@l1 P Y : l1  l
Especially we will say that l is a least upper bound of Y and we will denote it
as  Y , whenever l0 is another upper bound of Y then l  l0. Similarly we will
say that l P L is a lower bound of Y if
@l1 P Y : l  l1
we will say that l is a greatest lower bound of Y and we will denote it as  Y , if
for every other lower bound l0 of L we have l0  l. It is important to note that
it is not necessary for a subset Y of L to have least upper bound or greatest
lower bound.
Fact 1. For a subset Y a of partially ordered set pL, q, if  Y or  Y exist
then they are unique.
Proof. Assume that l1, l2 P L are both least upper bounds of Y . Then by the
denition of the least upper bound we have that for every other bound l0 P L
of Y we have that l1  l0 and l2  l0. Thus we have that
==PAGE:23==

11
l1  l2 ^ l2  l1 ñ l1  l2 ( antisymmetric)
Similarly for the greatest lower bound.
Complete Lattice. A complete lattice pL, q is a partially ordered set pL, q
such that all subsets Y of L have least and upper bounds.
Fact 2. If pL, q is a complete lattice then it has a least and a greatest element.
Proof. By denition we know that if pL, q is a complete lattice, we have that
every subset Y of L have least and upper bounds. Thus since L is a subset of
L, it has least and upper bounds. Therefore if l   L we know that
@l1 P L: l1  l (1)
and for every other upper bound l0 P L of L it holds that
l  l0 (2)
Thus by (1) and (2) and because  is antisymmetric we get that l1  l0. There-
fore l   L is the greatest element of L and we will denote it as J.
The proof is similarly for the least element and we will denote it as K. Further-
more we dene that K   H and J   H.

(i) L is a complete lattice,
(ii) every subset of L has a least upper bound and
(iii) every subset of L has a greatest upper bound
are equivalent.

2.3
Program states as a pair of an environment
and a memory
The rst way of representing a program's state as a σ P State  Var ÞÑ Var is
not suitable for programming languages that consist of more complex variable
types than simple variables (integers, booleans etc). Such complex variable types
could be pointers. In order to handle pointers we will represent a program's
state as a pair of an environment and a memory. Now let Var to be a nite
set of variable identiers and the set Ptr to be a nite set that includes the
pointer identiers. An environment then will be a mapping from the variables
set Id=Ptr Y Var to a location l P Loc, where Loc is nite , while a memory
will be a mapping from locations of the set Loc to an element of the set Val Y
Loc. Formally an environment is
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Program states as a pair of an environment and a memory
23
Env=Id Ñ Loc
and a memory is
Memory=Loc Ñ Val Y Loc
mapping a location of a variable to a value in the set Val and a location of a
pointer to a location in the set Loc. For instance let Var=tx, yu, Ptr=tptru
and Loc=tl1, l2, l3u and take Val=Z.
Then an example of an environment,
memory pair could be penv, mq where
env  rx ÞÑ l1, y ÞÑ l2, ptr ÞÑ l3s
m  rl1 ÞÑ 0, l2 ÞÑ 1, l3 ÞÑ l1s
The values of every variable and every pointer are determined by composing the
memory m with the environment env. Hence for our example we can compute
the value of x as mpenvpxqq  mpl1q  0; and for the pointer ptr we have
mpenvpptrqq  mpl3q  l1, meaning that the pointer ptr points to the location
of the variable x.

2.6
Content dependent information ow policies
Now we are ready to specify our content dependent information ow policies
for integrity.
As in the DLM we will have a nite set of security principals
Pr and then for a set of variables, a security policy will express who of the
security principals owns those variables, and who may inuence the value of
those variables. We will call those security principals owners and inuencers
respectively.
2.6.1
Syntax
A policy P P PolpL, V q is dened by the following syntax:
P :: t¯u : ¯o Ð ¯su | ˆϕ ñ P | P1  P2
We will write ¯x for a set of variables, while we denote the H as ϵ and the
singleton set as x. Therefore the basic policy t¯u : ¯o Ð ¯su, where ¯u is a subset
of the variables set V , expresses that the owners ¯o  Pr of ¯u, believe that the
inuencers ¯s  Pr may inuence the value of the variables in ¯u.
Next the
policy ˆϕ ñ P species a content dependent policy , expressing that the policy
P applies under the condition ˆϕ P L; where L is a nite, atomistic complete
lattice . Finally P1  P2 is the composition of two policies, where it expresses
that both of the policies have to be imposed and hence its inuencers are the
aggregate of the inuencers of the policy P1 and the inuencers of the policy P2.
The choice of the policy lattice L will be a total function space mapping vari-
ables to its best property. Let now pα, γq : PpSq è L to be an atomistic Galois
insertion and as we said before L is an atomistic complete lattice. For our ap-
proach we will either take S to be the set of a program's states (either State
or Pair), while later we will show how we can specify S to be a set of channel
states (mappings of channel variables to values).
Example 10. Let now Pr=tS1, S2u, V=tx, yu and L  V ÞÑ Signs where
Signs is the signs lattice of Example 1. Then we have the policy
==PAGE:43==
2.6 Content dependent information ow policies
31
P  rx ÞÑ 0, y ÞÑ 0s ñ tx : S1 Ð S2u
which expresses that S1 believes that S2 may inuence the value of the variable
x, when the sign of x is zero or minus and the the sign of y is zero.

Chapter 3
The While language
In this chapter, we will show how we can enforce content dependent informa-
tion ow policies on a sequential language While, which supports the standard
statements as the skip statement, assignments ,iteration and conditionals.
The chapter starts with the syntax and the semantics of While. We continue
with the denition of a static analysis specication and of an admissible result
based on our specication. Next, we specify when a specication of a static
analysis is correct with respect to a Galois insertion and to While's semantics.
Putting everything together, we dene our enforcement mechanism that consists
of a set of rules that specify when an admissible result of a correct specication
of static analysis for a program, is secure with respect to a content dependent
information ow policy.
Finally, we give our main theorem that states that
whenever a program is secure with respect to a content dependent policy then
the policy is correctly enforced on the information ow.
3.1
Syntax of the While language
The While language has as basic types booleans and arithmetic expressions. The
syntax of the arithmetic expressions e and the boolean expressions b is given by :
==PAGE:48==
36
The While language
e
:: n | x | e1 opa e2
b
:: true | false | e1 opr e2 | b1 opb b2 |  b
The basic elements of the arithmetic expressions e are integers n P Val (usually
we will take Val=Z) and variables x P Var, where Var is nite . The composite
elements of the arithmetic expressions e are constructed using the binary oper-
ator opa P t , , u. For the boolean expressions b, the basic elements are the
values true, false and e1 opr e2, where opr P t,  ,  ,  ¡u. The composite
element of the booleans is the negation operator  b. Recall that we we write
¯u for a set of variables and we use the greek letter ϵ to represent the empty
set of variables, while we write u to represent the set tuu, and also u1u2 for
the union of two sets u1, u2. Finally we will write ¯e and ¯b, for the set of free
variables which occur into an arithmetic expression e and a boolean expression
b, respectively; hence it is straightforward that ¯n  ϵ, ¯x  x, e1 opa e2  ¯e1 ¯e2,
while the denition for the boolean expressions is similar.
A program in our while language is a statement or a sequence of statements,
where the statements S consist of the usual skip, assignment, conditional and
loop constructs:
S
:: skip | x : e | S1; S2 | if b then S1 else S2 | while b do S
Example 15. An example program in our while language is the following which
assigns the value of y to z1 whenever the sign of y is positive and it assigns the
value of y to z2 whenever the sign of y is zero or negative.
y:=1; while true do (if y ¤ 0 then z2 : y else z1 : y ; y : 1  y;)
3.2
Semantics on Program Graphs
In order to work with various static analysis, we require a graph representation
for our programs. A program graph is a graph data structure, where its nodes
correspond to dierent program points and its edges correspond to the dierent
actions that transfer control from one program point to another. Formally a
program graph is a tuple :
pQ, A, E, q, qq
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Semantics on Program Graphs
37
where
Q is a nite set of nodes
A Act is a non-empty nite set of actions
E  pQ  A  Qq is a non-empty nite set of edges annotated with actions from A

The transitions are labelled with information about how the variables inu-

F  Var  Pr  Var

while a triple of the form py, s, q records that the value of the variable y is used

Chapter 4
Distributed Systems
In this chapter we give the syntax and the semantics of a language which sup-
ports synchronous communication between processes; and with that we give the
denition of a distributive system. The rest of the chapter follows the same
pattern as Chapter 3 and hence we give the semantics for a system based on a
system program graph, we next dene a static analysis on the system program
graph and then we show how we can enforce content dependent information ow
control policies on it.
4.1
Distributive System
. A distributive system Sys is described as a nite set of parallel processes :
Sys :
p1 : Sp1 ||...|| pn : Spn
Every process is identied by a unique name p and its body Sp. The syntax of
processes body S is given by :
S
::
skip | x : e | if b then S1 else S2 | while b do S | S1; S2
|
ch!e1..ek | ch?x1..xk | S1 ` S2
==PAGE:70==
58
Distributed Systems
The statements syntax has been extended with synchronous input and output data
communication over channels and with the non-deterministic choice S1 ` S2.
Every channel has a unique name ch P Ch and an arity , arity(ch)¡ 0; we use
channel variables #i to refer to the i1th position of the data communicated over a
channel and we write Varch 
 
#1, ..., #aritypchq

arity(ch)=k we demand that every occurrence of the channel statement in a
process will be of the form ch?x1...xk or ch!e1..ek and also that @i  j : xi  xj.
For the processes now we will write Proc for the set of the processes names
which occur in a system Sys.
We write Var=
pPProc Varp for the disjoint
union of sets of variables of the processes which means that the processes in Sys
do not have any shared variables. We also write Var   Var Y Var# for the
set Var extended with channel variables. Finally we shall assume that there is
a nite set of security principals Pr H. We will usually write  for the set
Pr.

The rules for the assignment and the condition are the same as they were in

input now we work similarly as the channel output considering the assignments
xi : #i. However the policy modications have to be split into two, since the

relevant substitution have to be applied on the policy Pch, while the implicit
ows from the variables in ¯y to a variable xi are local to the process and hence
the relevant substitution has to be applied on the policy Pp.

==PAGE:89==
Chapter 5
A language with pointers
In this chapter we extend the language for the distributive systems, with point-
ers. Pointers introduce new type of ows. We describe what a pointer-induced
ow is and we illustrate it through dierent examples. The rest of the chapter
is organised as chapter 3 and 4, however our enforcement mechanism is able to
take care only of programs that satisfy a certain condition.
5.1
Syntax
We extend our language to include pointer variables; we will write Ptr for the
set of the pointer identiers and we will call as simple variable, any variable in
the set Var. As it will be explained later the semantics will use a memory which
consists of locations and every location is characterized either as a location cell
(pointer variables) or as a value cell (simple variables), depending on the type
of information that it carries. The syntax of the expressions is now extended
with the following rules
lc
:: ptr | &x
e
:: n | vc | e1 opa e2
vc
:: x | ptr
==PAGE:90==
78
A language with pointers
The rule lc (location cell) consists of expressions that return a location; we write
&x for the location of the variable x P Var and ptr P Ptr is a pointer identi-
er. The rule vc (value cell) consists of the simple variable identiers x P Var
and the dereference action ptr which returns the value of a value cell that the
pointer ptr points to . We will write
¯
ptr  tptr, ptru and ¯
&x  ϵ.
The syntax of the statements is now :
S
:: ...| ptr : lc | ptr : e | ch?vc1...vcn
and recall that in the case of the channel input statement ch?vc1...vcn, a pro-
gram stops its execution if two value cells refer to the same location. However
the rejection of such a program is not as easy as before, and it can't be done just
by looking at the syntax of the program (e.g before, a program which includes
the statement ch?xx would have been rejected). A value cell vc now could be of
the form ptr and hence the location of the value cell vc is equal to the pointers
value ptr. For instance a program that includes the statement ch?ptrptr can
be rejected just by looking at the syntax of the program, but what about a
program that includes the statement ch?ptr1ptr2? The two pointers ptr1 and
ptr2 may point to the same value cell during the execution of the program and
hence our program will stop its execution. Therefore, since avionics major goal
is the safety of their system, we will not deal with programs that, their static
analysis result give us that two value cells may have the same location during
the execution of the program. We will give a formal denition of this statement
later.

In case of a simple variable, the token , a channel variable or a pointer vari-
able, the function C is the identity function, while in case of ptr it returns the
variable x that the pointer ptr points to the location of x. Recall here that we
will not consider null pointers and thus a pointer will always point to exactly
one variable x P Var . Before we give examples that illustrate how the dierent
ows are created and how the semantics work we will rst explain the new type
of ows that occur due to the pointer dereference action ptr.
5.5
Pointer-induced ows
A pointer-induced ow is created whenever an expression ptr is called . To
illustrate what a pointer-induced ow is consider the assignment x : ptr.
There are two type of ows here; the rst one is an explicit ow from the vari-
able that the pointer ptr points to, to the variable x, and the second one is a
pointer-induced ow from the pointer ptr to the variable x. The second ow
occurs because the attacker might learn the value of the pointer ptr if he pos-
sesses the location of the variable x. For instance if the value of x is 5 after the
assignment x : ptr, then the attacker knows that the pointer ptr points to a
location with the value 5.
In the case of the condition statement if ptr ¡ 0 then x : 0 else skip, we
have an implicit ow from the location that the pointer ptr points to, to the
variable x and also a pointer-induced ow from the pointer ptr to the variable
x. Again the second ow is created due to the case of the attacker possessing
the location of x and hence, if after the execution of the if statement the value
of x was 0 then he might learn that the pointer ptr points to a location with
value greater than 0.
In general whenever a ptr expression appears in a boolean condition or in
the right side of an assignment we generate two ows; the rst one is from the
location that the pointer ptr points to and the second one is from the pointer
ptr. This explains our choice of dening
¯
ptr  tptr, ptru.
Finally we have a pointer-induced ow whenever the left side of an assignment
==PAGE:97==
5.5 Pointer-induced ows
85
is a ptr expression. Consider the assignment ptr : x. Again here, there are
two type of information ow; the rst one is an implicit ow from the variable x
to the location that the pointer ptr points to, while the second one is a pointer-
induced ow from pointer ptr to the location that the pointer ptr points to. As
before, the second ow occurs due to the case in which the attacker possesses the
location l that the pointer ptr points to; hence after the assignment ptr  1,
if l has the value 1 then the attacker knows that the pointer ptr points to a
location with value 1.
Let us now see some examples to understand how the dierent ows are cre-
ated. First of all notice that whenever there is not a pointer dereference ptr
expression in a statement S, then the function that creates the ows works as
before, since C is the identity function.
For instance consider the following example assignment x : z; there is an
implicit ow from the variable z to x. Both z and x are in the set Id  and
thus Crrxsspenv, mq  x and Crrzsspenv, mq  z. Hence for some s P Pr the ow
pz, s, xq is recorded correctly. The same will happen in the case of a pointer
assignment; Consider the assignment p1 : p2, where p1, p2 P Ptr. There is
an information ow from the pointer variable p2 to the pointer variable p1.
Since both p1 and p2 belong to the set Id  we have that Crrp1sspenv, mq  p1
and Crrp2sspenv, mq  p2; hence we have the expected ow pp2, s, p1q for some

The function for the assignment action updates the abstract memory of the
variables by mapping the location of x to the abstract value of the expression e;
==PAGE:107==
5.7 Static Analysis for Pointers
95
while the function for the condition works as before. Next, the function for the
pointer assignment, updates the abstract memory for the pointers by mapping
the location of the pointer ptr to the abstract location of the location cell lc.
The function for the pointer dereference action assignment works as the function
for the assignment action when the pointer ptr points exactly to one abstract
location of a variable x. Otherwise if the pointer ptr points to more than one
location, then we do not perform a strong update and we keep track of the
previous values that the variables that ptr may point to had. The function for
the channel output action works as in Chapter 4. Finally, the function for the
channel input action produces a new abstract memory for the variables and it
is dened recursively on the channel's ch arity k. If the channel has only one
variable e.g ch?x then the value of the variable x is updated by the channels
variable κp#1q. Now there is also the case that we could have ch?ptr, where the
pointer ptr may points to one or more locations, hence if the pointer points to
one variable we perform a strong update as before, otherwise we have to update
all the variables that ptr may points to, but we also need to keep track of the
previous values that those variables had. Now assume that the channel ch has
arity k ¡ 1. We rst need to compute the abstract memory pσk1 and then the
abstract memory pσk. This happens due to the reason that more than one value
cells that appear on the channel input ch?vc1...vck may be pointers and hence
they may point to the same variable. Let us see an example to understand the
functionality of the function for the channel input.
Assume that we have the channel input statement ch?ptr1xptr2 and let pκ P

of variables to another set of variables. In case of ¯x being the singleton set,
then the strongest substitution behaves as the normal substitution. In the case
now that |¯x| ¡ 1 we will either add all the variables of ¯x to the policy P or we
will leave P unchanged. For instance this will be useful when we will have an
assignment and the left side of it is the pointer dereference action ptr. Thus
as we know our analysis result may give us that the pointer ptr may point to
more than one variable and hence all of those variables should be added to P, if
the "basic" body of a policy P includes variables from the set ¯y. On the other
hand if the result of our analysis pπ gives that the pointer ptr may points to more
than one variable, and hence pπplptrq  tx1, ..., xnu; but there are no occurrences
of the variables of ¯y in the basic body of P then we should not remove all of
the variables of pπplptrq from P. We formulate some properties of the strongest
substitution with the following fact:

==PAGE:121==
Chapter 6
Implementation
A concrete implementation of a tool that uses the enforcement mechanisms
that presented in Chapter 4 and 5 has been made in the functional program-
ming language F#.
The choice of F# as the implementation language was
made due to the nature of this project, since it includes many mathematical
denitions of functions and algebraic structures; hence the specication of them
in a functional language is much easier than doing it in a non-functional one.
The implementation includes a parser for the pointer language of Chapter 5, and
it has been created using the lexer fslex and the parser creator fsyacc [Pag].
Since the language which was presented in Chapter 4 is a subset of the language
which was presented in Chapter 5, there was no need of creating an extra parser.
Next we will briey describe the "interesting" functions of our implementation.
6.1
Security Policies
We have developed a general framework in which someone could specify content
dependent information policies for integrity. The denitions of the strongest
postcondition function, while also the substitution function, are standard. The
ordering function ¨L,V of the policies has ve arguments; two policies P1, P2 P
PolpL, V 1q, a set V of variables, and nally a function that returns the atoms
==PAGE:122==
110
Implementation
of a ˆϕ P L while also the ordering function of the lattice L.
6.2
The Enforcement Mechanisms
We have developed a general framework for both of our enforcement mecha-
nisms. Both of them have 20 arguments
1. A program graph.
2. Two mappings, where the rst maps every node of the program graph to
a static analysis result, while the second maps every channel's name to a
static analysis result.
3. Two mappings, where the rst maps every process name to a policy, while
the second maps every channel's name to a policy.
4. Three specication of monotone functions, one for the local actions of the
processes, one for the channel input actions and one for the channel output
actions.
5. Four functions α1, γ1, α1
ch, γ1
ch, that represent the functions of the Galois
insertions between the static analysis result and the policies. Note that
for the channels we do not have to specify an α1
ch and γ1
ch for each channel
since all those functions have the same functionality.
6. Two orderings one for the channels policy lattice and one for the processes
policy lattice.
7. Two functions that return the atoms of an element of the channels policy
lattice and one that returns the atoms of the processes policy lattice.
8. One greatest lower bound operator corresponding to the lattice that our
analysis result uses, while also a mapping that maps every channel to a
greatest lower bound operator.
9. A set ID which includes all the variable identiers that appear in a pro-
gram.
The tool veries a program by checking the rules of the enforcement mechanisms
that have been specied on Chapter 4 and 5. Whenever there is an invalid ow
on a program graph's edge, the relevant information is showed on the screen.
==PAGE:123==
6.3 Tests
111
6.3
Tests
We have tested our tool on our getaway example (Example 29) and it has been
veried. The output of the tool can be seen in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Output for no invalid ows
Now we also made a test in which we have substituted the condition on the two
policies of interest of the demultiplexer and hence we dened
Pd 
prx0 ÞÑ J, x1 ÞÑ J, y ÞÑ  , z ÞÑ Jsq ñ tz :  Ð P0u

prx0 ÞÑ J, x1 ÞÑ J, y ÞÑ 0, z ÞÑ Jsq ñ tz :  Ð P1u

prx0 ÞÑ J, x1 ÞÑ J, y ÞÑ J, z ÞÑ Jsq ñ ty :  Ð mu
The results of our tool are given in Figure 6.2. The output of the tool, rstly
states the edge in which the invalid ow has been occurred. Next, depending
on the rule of our enforcement mechanism that has not been satised, it shows
the left and the right policy of the rule. Last but not least it shows the reason
for which the rule was not satised.

==PAGE:125==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
Ensuring safety in embedded systems, and particularly in avionics has been the
major goal until now.
However, in industries such as the aerospace, the in-
troduction of new and complex systems that communicate through computer
networks, calls for new challenges that concern the security of those systems.
One of those challenges is the enforcement of content dependent information
ow policies, that ensure data integrity.
In this thesis, we developed a mechanism that enforces content dependent in-
formation ow policies for integrity using Abstract Interpretation. We started
in Chapter 2 by giving the required background on Abstract Interpretation and
then we specied content dependent information ow policies, making use of
atomistic Galois Insertions and nite atomistic complete lattices. In Chapter
3 we considered a simple sequential language and we gave its semantics based
on program graphs, where a program graph edge is annotated with information
ows. Next, we gave the denition of a correct static analysis specication I
and we specied a decidable mechanism that enforces content dependent in-
formation ow policies with the use of an admissible result with respect to I.
Finally, we concluded, by giving a theorem that expresses the correctness of our
enforcement mechanism and we formally proved our theorem. In Chapter 4 we
extended the language of Chapter 3, with channels that allow synchronous com-
munication between two or more processes. We then gave the formal denition
of a Distributive System, and then the rest of the chapter followed the same
==PAGE:126==
114
Conclusion
pattern as Chapter 3, nishing with the correctness of our enforcement mech-
anism . In Chapter 5 we added pointers that point to simple variables, to the
language of Chapter 4 and again the whole chapter followed the same pattern as
Chapter 5. However our enforcement mechanism here, can verify systems that
their admissible result satisfy a certain condition that concerns the safety of the
system. Finally, in Chapter 6 we briey described a concrete implementation of
a tool that imposes content dependent information ow control with the use of
the enforcement mechanisms of Chapter 5 and 6, while we also show our results
of our tool, after testing it on an example from the aerospace industry. The tool
has been implemented in the functional programming language F#.
As future work, we could consider several directions. One could be the speci-
cation of content dependent information ow policies for condentiality . We
believe that this would be an easy task, and it can be done by adapting the
Hoare logic approach of [HRN16] for the condentiality policies into our ap-
proach, working similarly as we did with the integrity policies. A more chal-
lenging direction could be the extension of our pointer language with commands
of the C language as the ones that have been considered in [Mac15], while also
extensions that include functions and procedures. Every one of those extensions
that discussed before will be followed by a relative extension in our tool. Finally,
a formal documentation of our tool's architecture should be made.
